<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_9d79aa1a-621d-40ac-b3a1-b46da513881d'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

<span id="freeTextContainer10778120514581900718">To his friends, to his coworkers, and even to his mistress Moira, Roy Dillon is an honest hardworking salesman.   <span id="freeText10778120514581900718" style="display:none">To his friends, to his coworkers, and even to his mistress Moira, Roy Dillon is an honest hardworking salesman. He lives in a cheap hotel just within his pay bracket. He goes to work every day. He has hundreds of friends and associates who could attest to his good character.<br /><br />Yet, hidden behind three gaudy clown paintings in Roy's pallid hotel room, sits fifty-two thousand dollars--the money Roy makes from his short cons, his "grifting." For years, Roy has effortlessly maintained control over his house-of-cards life--until the simplest con goes wrong, and he finds himself critically injured and at the mercy of the most dangerous woman he ever met: his own mother.<br /><br />THE GRIFTERS, one of the best novels ever written about the art of the con, is an ingeniously crafted story of deception and betrayal that was the basis for Stephen Frears' and Martin Scorsese's critically-acclaimed film of the same name.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10778120514581900718" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="bookReviews">
  




  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_169381126" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/169381126" />
    <a title="Stephen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311616811p2/1413439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/169381126?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen">Stephen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679732488" href="/book/show/592676.The_Grifters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=hardboiled-and-noir">hardboiled-and-noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=1954-1969">1954-1969</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer169381126" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12253026036990994845">For all you <b>Petey Positives</b> and <b>Betty Bright sides</b> out there peering around corners looking for humanity’s better angels, <b>Jim Thompson</b> is slinging a sledge hammer ready to shatter your <b>shiny happy </b> illusions. His message: people well and truly suck. Personally, I’m enough of a cynic regarding my fellow humans that Jimmy’s words don't choke me going down. They're like 18 year old scotch warming up my <b>cockles</b>. <br/><br/>In this <b>“slice the vein of life”</b> story, Jim introduces us to 3 peas in a seriously <b>fucko</b></span>
  <span id="freeText12253026036990994845" style="display:none">For all you <b>Petey Positives</b> and <b>Betty Bright sides</b> out there peering around corners looking for humanity’s better angels, <b>Jim Thompson</b> is slinging a sledge hammer ready to shatter your <b>shiny happy </b> illusions. His message: people well and truly suck. Personally, I’m enough of a cynic regarding my fellow humans that Jimmy’s words don't choke me going down. They're like 18 year old scotch warming up my <b>cockles</b>. <br /><br />In this <b>“slice the vein of life”</b> story, Jim introduces us to 3 peas in a seriously <b>fuckoed</b> pod. We have Roy Dillon, a 25 year old, smart, savvy, small time con artist with more mother issues than <i>Norman Bates</i>. Roy's a very successful short con operator who suffers a major set back at the beginning of story when “a mark” detects his scam and gives him what for in the stomach with a heavy club...HARD. <br /><br />Next we have <i>“Roy’s mother issue”</i> herself, Lilly Dillon, who, at 39 years old (she had Roy at 14), has been grifting longer than Roy’s been breathing. Lilly works for a big time mobster named Bobo Justus, where she travels to horse races and places bets on “long shots” for the big gambling syndicates in order to bring the odds down. <br /><br />Finally, we have Moira Langtry, Roy’s older girl-friend, who bears an eerie resemblance to Lilly and is quite a <b>shitty little person</b> in her own right. Moira used to work a two man “long con” for big money and is now searching for a new partner (Hmm…any guesses?). In the meantime, she gets by trading sex for rent, food, drinks and other assorted sundries. <br /><br />Well, these 3 sharks in designer clothes all come together when Roy’s tummy trouble turns out to be rather serious and gets him hospitalized. <u>Enter Carol Roberg</u>: part-time nurse at the hospital and the one ray of bright light in this sordid American tale. Carol is kind, gentle, generous, pretty, very naive and harbors a painfully dark secret that I won’t spoil here (however I warn you that I audibly gasped when I found out). <br /><br />Carol becomes a chunk of wood between the twin buzz saws of Lilly and Roy, with Moiry in the corner holding a jar of salt and some lemon juice for the clean up. However, compared to what the three do to themselves and each other, Carol ends up getting off comparatively light. <br /><br />This story is bleak. It's subtle, yet savage and spends all its time showing us the lives of 3 people who, combined, have less heart than the Grinch before the “Whos” started caroling. What they do to one another is sometimes tough to watch, but when a good, normal person crosses their path, it's like a feeding frenzy. <br /><br />HOWEVER, what's most amazing about Thompson’s story is that he has you constantly on the edge of “feeling something” for these characters, especially Roy, with whom we spend the most page time. Roy is scarred and damaged from upbringing with Lilly, but there are shreds of the good person still inside, the person who wants to lead a normal life and do the right thing. There were several times while reading this that my mind was screaming at the page for Roy to take one road rather than another...the guy killed me. <br /><br />This is what Thompson is so good at, literary sadist that he is. He makes you “feel” for his characters. I won’t call it pity or even understanding, but more a hope that they are about to listen to the fading image of their conscience and do the right thing. Thompson has a real gift for showing you very dark, damaged people and getting you to see through their eyes. He doesn’t want you to agree with what they do and he isn’t looking to justify their actions, but he can make their inhuman cruelty seem very, very human. <br /><br />Of course, this kind of writing rips me up inside and makes me want to <b>slap him in the sack with a black jack</b> because of it. He deserves it, believe me. <br /><br />One last thing...the end is AMAZING and will absolutely floor you. You've been warned!!<br /><br />Overall, this was dark and depressing, and I loved it. The only thing that keeps me from giving it 5 stars is that I'd previously seen the movie, with John Cusack, Angelica Huston and Annette Bening, about 5 times before reading this (I obviously loved it) and so the suspense was a bit watered down. Those that haven’t yet seen the movie, I would recommend reading the book first and then watching the film. You will love both. <br /><br />4.5 stars. HIGHEST POSSIBLE RECOMMENDATION!!!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12253026036990994845" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/15989491-the-grifters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=2015">2015</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1380936868" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer485230159115667369">Salesman Roy Dillon has a secret life as a top shelf grifter. When he has the opportunity to go legit, how will the women in his life take it? Will his downfall be at the hands of his girlfriend Moira Langtry, or his mother, Lilly?<br/><br/>Here we are, another noir tale of self-destruction by Mr. Happy, Jim Thompson.<br/><br/>"Don't trust anyone ever" seems to be the moral of The Grifters. Not surprising since most of the main characters are shady croooks and grifters to some degree. Roy runs short cons. Moira Lan</span>
  <span id="freeText485230159115667369" style="display:none">Salesman Roy Dillon has a secret life as a top shelf grifter. When he has the opportunity to go legit, how will the women in his life take it? Will his downfall be at the hands of his girlfriend Moira Langtry, or his mother, Lilly?<br /><br />Here we are, another noir tale of self-destruction by Mr. Happy, Jim Thompson.<br /><br />"Don't trust anyone ever" seems to be the moral of The Grifters. Not surprising since most of the main characters are shady croooks and grifters to some degree. Roy runs short cons. Moira Langtry pays her way through life with her body. Lilly Dillon is mixed up with some shady gamblers. Not one of them should be left unsupervised.<br /><br />Roy's a little more sympathetic than most Jim Thompson leads. He's conflicted about his grifting lifestyle and just seems tired. Not only that, he's got serious mommy issues, nicely illustrated by the resemblance between his girlfriend and his mother. <br /><br />The Grifters is a slow burn and the ending is pretty spectacular, cold, violent, and more than a little creepy. I've seen similar endings written since but I don't think any of them were as effective. <br /><br />This is a top shelf Jim Thompson book, belonging on the same tier as The Killer Inside Me and Pop. 1280. Four out of five stars.</span>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/758855865" />
    <a title="Cbj" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5283293-cbj"><img alt="Cbj" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579249711p2/5283293.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/758855865?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cbj" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cbj" href="/user/show/5283293-cbj">Cbj</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679732488" href="/book/show/592676.The_Grifters">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer758855865" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer292533010755337552">I'm not a big Jim Thompson fan. When it comes to American crime fiction, I prefer the novels of Charles Willeford and Charles Williams over the ones by Thompson. I am often irritated by Thompson's lack of attention to detail, over the top dialog and faux vicious and corrupt characters. I hated <b>The Killer Inside Me</b> and only enjoyed parts of <b>The Getaway</b>. <br/><br/>But <b>The Grifters</b> was quite impressive. <br/><br/>Roy Dillon is an undercover conman who is trying to escape from the incestuous clutches of his mother Lily</span>
  <span id="freeText292533010755337552" style="display:none">I'm not a big Jim Thompson fan. When it comes to American crime fiction, I prefer the novels of Charles Willeford and Charles Williams over the ones by Thompson. I am often irritated by Thompson's lack of attention to detail, over the top dialog and faux vicious and corrupt characters. I hated <b>The Killer Inside Me</b> and only enjoyed parts of <b>The Getaway</b>. <br /><br />But <b>The Grifters</b> was quite impressive. <br /><br />Roy Dillon is an undercover conman who is trying to escape from the incestuous clutches of his mother Lily Dillon while also trying to choose between his lover Moira Langtry (<i>who looks a lot like his mother</i>) and the innocent Jewish nurse Carol Roberg even as he recovers from an internal hemorrhage picked up during a minor con job. <br /><br />Every character has a backstory that is narrated through flashback. Moira Langtry's flashback was excellent. So were the descriptions of Roy Dillon's methods.<br /><br />The book suffers from some of the irritations that I mentioned at the start. But Thompson keeps it entertaining throughout and throws in a couple of glorious twists towards the end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="292533010755337552" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_905118095" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/905118095" />
    <a title="Mara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1923002-mara"><img alt="Mara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589463455p2/1923002.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/905118095?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mara" href="/user/show/1923002-mara">Mara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679732488" href="/book/show/592676.The_Grifters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1923002-mara?shelf=2014-reads">2014-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1923002-mara?shelf=black-lizard">black-lizard</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1923002-mara?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1923002-mara?shelf=library-books">library-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1923002-mara?shelf=codename-duchess">codename-duchess</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer905118095" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8321593187615480640"><blockquote>
  <em>
    <strong>There was one thing about playing the angles. If you played them long enough, you knew the other guy's as well as you knew your own. Most of the time it was like you were looking out the same window.</strong>
  </em>
</blockquote><br/>Roy Dillon, grifter extraordinaire, was always playing the angles. Though Roy is undoubtedly our protagonist, this really is more of an ensemble piece. While <a title='The Killer Inside Me' href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/298663.The_Killer_Inside_Me' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Killer Inside Me</a> and <a title='Pop. 1280' href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/118149.Pop_1280' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>Pop. 1280</a> are meditations on (if you can call such depraved tales “meditations”) the existence of one twis</span>
  <span id="freeText8321593187615480640" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <em>
    <strong>There was one thing about playing the angles. If you played them long enough, you knew the other guy's as well as you knew your own. Most of the time it was like you were looking out the same window.</strong>
  </em>
</blockquote><br>Roy Dillon, grifter extraordinaire, was always playing the angles. Though Roy is undoubtedly our protagonist, this really is more of an ensemble piece. While <a title="The Killer Inside Me" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/298663.The_Killer_Inside_Me" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Killer Inside Me</a> and <a title="Pop. 1280" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/118149.Pop_1280" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Pop. 1280</a> are meditations on (if you can call such depraved tales “meditations”) the existence of one twisted mind amid a world filled with easy marks and clueless rubes, Roy is not a lone fox in a hen-house. His mother, Lilly, and paramour, Moira, are stars in acts of their own, giving rise to deliciously unpredictable interactions where you're never quite sure who is playing who(m).<br><br>This wasn't my first ticket to the <a title="Jim Thompson Goodreads" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7621.Jim_Thompson" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Jim Thompson</a> show, so while I was expecting a level of "darkness" that begs for a stronger word than simply <strong>
  <em>noir</em>
</strong>, I was not expecting was the elegance of it all. It wasn't so much that the twists simply surprised me, or proved me right or wrong- they brought a level of near-existential reflection and depth that gave a sort of bonus gravitas to an already well-written, highly-recommended, riveting read about ever-precarious life on "Uneasy Street."<br><br><blockquote>
<strong><em>For a fearful shadow lies constantly over the residents of Uneasy Street. It casts itself through the ostensibly friendly handshake, or the gorgeously wrapped package. It beams out from the baby's carriage, the barber's chair, the beauty parlor. Every neighbor is suspect, every outsider, everyone period; even one's own husband or wife or sweetheart. There is no ease on Uneasy Street. The longer one's tenancy, the more untenable it becomes.</em></strong> </blockquote><br>9/10 stars, but this one definitely deserved to be rounded up for Goodreads. <br><br><br><strong>Bonus thoughts and/or comments:</strong><br><br>I, for one, didn't know what a punchboard was, so I figured I'd share with the class.<br><br><a title="Punchboard" href="http://imgur.com/IQEoHvW" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1397237088i/9252194.png" alt="Punchboard" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Also, as severe blows to the abdomen play a role in the story, my thoughts quickly turned to Harry Houdini and thus (yes, obviously), <em>
  <strong>Archer</strong>
</em>.<br><br><em>Cyril: I’m so sorry I slammed you in the gut. Jeez, that’s how Houdini died.</em><br><em>Pam: Houdini died of AIDS.</em><br><em>Cyril: No! Why do you always say that?</em><br><br><a title="That's how houdini died" href="http://imgur.com/Fa3Ha1B" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1397237088i/9252195.jpg" alt="That's how houdini died" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><em>Archer: Medical fact, Cyril.If you get hit in the chest between heartbeats, you can die.Go ask Houdini!<br>Pam (chuckling): Ask him what, how to get AIDS?</em></span>
  <a data-text-id="8321593187615480640" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_216151468" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/216151468" />
    <a title="Richard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4677640-richard"><img alt="Richard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472613597p2/4677640.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/216151468?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/4677640-richard">Richard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679732488" href="/book/show/592676.The_Grifters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=city-of-angels">city-of-angels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=author-jim-thompson">author-jim-thompson</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=noir-pulp-classics">noir-pulp-classics</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer216151468" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9505132572695821843">Ok guys, here goes. I started to read <i>The Grifters</i> and got about 70 pages in until I began to wonder whether or not I was reading the right book. Like maybe it was a novelization of the movie (which I haven't seen)? Because not only did I love the two other Jim Thompson books I've read previously, but so many people are fans of this particular book and it couldn't possibly be the one I was reading, right? I was expecting an entertaining con man thriller at worst, or another masterpiece of noir a</span>
  <span id="freeText9505132572695821843" style="display:none">Ok guys, here goes. I started to read <i>The Grifters</i> and got about 70 pages in until I began to wonder whether or not I was reading the right book. Like maybe it was a novelization of the movie (which I haven't seen)? Because not only did I love the two other Jim Thompson books I've read previously, but so many people are fans of this particular book and it couldn't possibly be the one I was reading, right? I was expecting an entertaining con man thriller at worst, or another masterpiece of noir at best. But what I was reading was a bland soap opera about a love triangle between a short-con artist, his mother and his girlfriend. Maybe if I knew that's what it was from the start, I would've enjoyed it... It just kept going back and forth about Roy's mommy issues, and his conflicting desires to leave grifting behind. I even looked up the Wikipedia synopsis to make sure that I wasn't missing anything. <br /><br />I wasn't. I was pretty uninterested, but I thought: Okay, this is building a set-up and then Thompson is gonna start putting it into overdrive in the 2nd half. Then the 2nd half of the book came and went. And it was more of the same, even up to the ending that people supposedly love. A big redeeming factor though is that Thompson's narrative voice is so enjoyable to read. That kept me going. But ultimately, I was disappointed that this one wasn't for me, and I'm most definitely in the very small minority. I thought the characters were easily forgettable and story itself pretty uneventful. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9505132572695821843" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_69532900" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/69532900" />
    <a title="Moira Russell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1630617-moira-russell"><img alt="Moira Russell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1256449807p2/1630617.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/69532900?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Moira Russell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Moira Russell" href="/user/show/1630617-moira-russell">Moira Russell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780752864280" href="/book/show/101221.The_Grifters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1630617-moira-russell?shelf=2009-50-new-books-challenge">2009-50-new-books-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer69532900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10458385565019063638">If people have read or even heard of this book, it's probably because of the movie -- which is a shame. I only bought this book because a movie tie-in was on sale for five bucks at a local Half Price and there was an evil character named Moira, which amused me, and it would take a far stronger character than mine to withstand those twin temptations. (I have not seen the movie, mainly because an evil John Cusack would throw my hard-wired <i>Say Anything/Grosse Pointe Blank</i> fangirl brane into disarra</span>
  <span id="freeText10458385565019063638" style="display:none">If people have read or even heard of this book, it's probably because of the movie -- which is a shame. I only bought this book because a movie tie-in was on sale for five bucks at a local Half Price and there was an evil character named Moira, which amused me, and it would take a far stronger character than mine to withstand those twin temptations. (I have not seen the movie, mainly because an evil John Cusack would throw my hard-wired <i>Say Anything/Grosse Pointe Blank</i> fangirl brane into disarray.) I'm supposed to be <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/69180137" rel="nofollow noopener">watching the pawns get marched out in the opening words of A.S. Byatt's <i>The Game,</i></a> but I read a few pages from the middle of this book, got hooked on the short, evocative, deceptively simple sentences, read a few sections more and flipped back to the beginning to start properly (yeah, I frequently read mysteries starting with the <i>end,</i> so I know whodunnit. Sometimes if I try reading mysteries from the beginning without knowing the end I can't properly focus on the story. It's a sickness, I know). <br /><br />Unfortunately it will be impossible to say anything about the spring-loaded plot, which keeps snapping shut on your fingers as you turn pages, without utterly spoiling it, so I won't. I will just say this: I have read <i>a lot</i> of gritty noir stories with trick climaxes and twist endings. This one <i>works,</i> because it's not just a showoff gotcha on the reader (no, I'm not STILL BITTER about Roger Ackroyd, thanks for asking). Surprises and deceptions are woven into the narrative like they're shot through the lives of the characters trapped in it. It all fits together with such bleak neatness it feels fated. <br /><br />Other people have called this book grim, depressing, nihilistic -- and sure, it is all these things -- but there's also a clean brutality about it that's almost exhilarating, like seeing the excellence displayed at the races that feature in one of the book's subplots. No, nobody really escapes and there's no happy ending (that can't really be a spoiler) but when someone sets down so much truth so well in such a short space all I can do is catch my breath and say: Wow.<br /><br />I love inside details about cons, but this book, despite some good descriptions of the lives of petty thieves and grifters, isn't really about that -- it's one more attempt at a portrait of Los Angeles, and pointedly at the end a character walks 'out of the room and the hotel, and out into the City of Angels.' The (anti)hero picks Los Angeles as a safe place to practice in after he spends too long in too small a section of New York out of ignorance, and the anonymous, fragmented, fractured, alienated and alienating life he shares with millions of others is the real point; Thompson details these atomistic existences carefully, without political or socioeconomic reproaches, just as damning and neutral as a Walker Evans Depression-era photo. <br /><br />You can see how someone could read it and might want to make a non-chronologically-straightforward movie like <i>Memento</i> out of it -- frequently a scene starts <i>in media res,</i> then there's a little flashback to an even earlier scene, and on top of that the scene we originally start with begins right after something big exploded and small remnants of dialogue from that event, in italics, punctuate the narrative -- like little bits of reported shrapnel. There are small neat precise little prose poems about the paranoia of small-time crooks, how to set up dice games, and so on -- but the story doesn't depend on technical accounts of the tricks, altho we get brief vivid details of the 'tat,' the 'twenty' and a few other schemes. The cons themselves work <i>because</i> of the nature of modern city life -- people are so alienated that they take the manufactured, counterfeit moments of intimacy that con games need to work ('con' isn't shortened from 'confidence' for nothing) as the real deal, which is what lets the grifters make the marks. The table the games run on isn't the scarred wood of a bar or a smooth green field of poker felt but, as always, the human heart. Emotions are what makes con games possible; a classic example of this is David Mamet's intricate <i>House of Games,</i> where being let in on the con is itself a form of the con. Several characters in this novel fantasize about setting up 'long' cons, as opposed to 'short' cons like tricking a harassed cashier out of change for a twenty, but the truth is in life no long con is possible at all. Cons depend on burning up human relationships, trust, ignorance, innocence, the very idea that the person smiling at you isn't sizing up your every tell, like jet engines eat rocket fuel. <br /><br />But there's another side to this that goes back far before David Mamet or counting cards, to Falstaff's mock-tragic cry when Prince Hal and Poins scheme within a scheme to set him up: <i>a plague upon it when thieves cannot be true one to another!</i> The novel's events inform us that just as cons can't stop conning even other cons, their actions make themselves marks in a way other people can't even imagine. In a neat little sequence, one man sees a punchboard in a bar and is taken aback, since they are one of the lowest, cheapest kinds of con: the person running the board punches out all the top prize slots, so noone ever has a chance to win. But this bartender has been a sucker, leaving his board clean, so the con starts running a game on him, rationalizing it's for the man's own good -- to tell him about the con itself would mean giving away 'knowledge no honest person should have,' and he thinks he'll instead refuse his winnings, saying the board must be defective, and get the bartender to toss it without realizing he's been duped. But just then, an undercover cop busts the bartender for not having a federal gambling tax-stamp, and the con man for running 'bunco.' The person who imagines they're running the game is, of course, the biggest sucker of all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10458385565019063638" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_256510675" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/256510675" />
    <a title="Jayaprakash Satyamurthy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1497559-jayaprakash-satyamurthy"><img alt="Jayaprakash Satyamurthy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1517076488p2/1497559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/256510675?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jayaprakash Satyamurthy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jayaprakash Satyamurthy" href="/user/show/1497559-jayaprakash-satyamurthy">Jayaprakash Satyamurthy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780752864280" href="/book/show/101221.The_Grifters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1497559-jayaprakash-satyamurthy?shelf=noir">noir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer256510675" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18377300227106461908">Noir? There's got to be something darker than that to describe Thompson's books. Something that doesn't just imply the absence of colour or light but the impossibility of their ever having existed in the first place. I liked this one a lot, even better than The Getaway though perhaps not as much as The Killer Inside Me. <br/><br/>Roy Dillon, like most of Thompson's protagonists, is young, charming and crooked. The son of a similarly charming and crooked con woman, he's been living in Los Angeles and worki</span>
  <span id="freeText18377300227106461908" style="display:none">Noir? There's got to be something darker than that to describe Thompson's books. Something that doesn't just imply the absence of colour or light but the impossibility of their ever having existed in the first place. I liked this one a lot, even better than The Getaway though perhaps not as much as The Killer Inside Me. <br /><br />Roy Dillon, like most of Thompson's protagonists, is young, charming and crooked. The son of a similarly charming and crooked con woman, he's been living in Los Angeles and working a shrewd set of short-term con jobs alongside the facade of a respectable life. But one day, he picks the wrong patsy and receives a blow to the stomach that causes internal bleeding. <br /><br />Vulnerable, he soon finds himself in a deadly love quadrilateral involving his seductive, self-serving mother Lilly, his equally alluring lover Myra, who has a few secrets he doesn't know yet and the innocent but scarred nurse, Carol. <br /><br />He's put through the wringer in this novel and he thinks he's decided to go straight by the time it's all over. But things never quite work that way in a Thompson novel and the brutality of the final two twists in the tale left me astonished and a little breathless. I would love to imagine that Thompson was writing about some other species on some other planet; perhaps the most terrifying thing about his novels is that they are fiction with the stamp of truth. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18377300227106461908" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2007620018" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2007620018" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2007620018?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781619690066" href="/book/show/17560798-the-grifters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=mystery-detective-thriller">mystery-detective-thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2007620018" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer566779342898069818">“A confidence trick is also known as a con game, a con, a scam, a grift, a hustle, a bunko (or bunco), a swindle, a flimflam, a gaffle or a bamboozle. The intended victims are known as "marks", "suckers", or "gulls" (i.e., gullible). When accomplices are employed, they are known as shills”—Wikipedia<br/><br/>To grift is to engage in petty swindling. <br/><br/>Until this week I had never read The Grifters (1963), but had seen several times the film with Jon Cusack, Annette Bening, and Anjelica Huston and loved it, a</span>
  <span id="freeText566779342898069818" style="display:none">“A confidence trick is also known as a con game, a con, a scam, a grift, a hustle, a bunko (or bunco), a swindle, a flimflam, a gaffle or a bamboozle. The intended victims are known as "marks", "suckers", or "gulls" (i.e., gullible). When accomplices are employed, they are known as shills”—Wikipedia<br /><br />To grift is to engage in petty swindling. <br /><br />Until this week I had never read The Grifters (1963), but had seen several times the film with Jon Cusack, Annette Bening, and Anjelica Huston and loved it, a classic. I recently read one of Thompson’s other classics, The Killer Inside Me (1952), that I found deliciously vicious, but admittedly at times a little too brutal. The Grifters dials down the viciousness and focuses on (mostly) the emotional states of short con grifter Roy, his long con grifter mother Lily, and Roy’s girlfriend (that looks very much like Lily) Moira, but it is equally delicious, in its own way. <br /><br />The Grifters, in spite of its pulpy themes, has a real literary foundation. Okay, he’s not Hemingway, but like many of the noir writers of the mid last century, he was influenced by him. He was also influenced by his favorite author, Dostoevsky, who like Thompson chronicled the down and out gamblers and alcoholics, the lost souls. Thompson was nicknamed the "Dimestore Dostoevsky" by writer Geoffrey O'Brien, who also found elements of Greek tragedy in Thompson’s themes. As with Dostoevsky, Thompson can be very clever and very funny, and surely is both in The Grifters.<br /><br />“Moira purred: ‘Gosh, Roy, I have no idea how I can ever thank you. . .’<br /><br />‘Hmm,’ Roy said, ‘Maybe I can think of something. Maybe you can wash my socks or something. . .”<br /><br />Okay, okay, maybe that's not that funny or clever, but hey, I smiled! But in general here Thompson calls on the darker side of Dostoevsky, focusing on the burned-out, fragile lives of Roy and Lily, who are just sick to death of the grift grindstone:<br /><br />“There was too much of a sameness about the evening’s delights. He had been the same route too many times. He’d been there before, so double-damned often, and however you traveled—backward, forward, or walking on your hands—you always got to the same place. You got nowhere, in other words, and each trip took a little more out of you.” <br /><br />And: <br /><br />“He [Roy] picked her [Moira] up and tossed her on the bed.<br /><br />They had a hell of a time.<br /><br />But afterward, after she had gone back to her own room, depression came to him and what had seemed like such a hell of a time became distasteful, even a little disgusting. It was the depression of surfeit, the tail of self-indulgence’s kite. You flew high, wide, and handsome, imposing on the breeze that might have wafted you along indefinitely; and then it was gone, and down, down, down you went.” <br /><br />And once again:<br /><br />“Strolling down a white-graveled walk to the cliff above the ocean, he let his eyes rove aimlessly over the expanse of sea and sand: The icy-looking whitecaps, the blinking, faraway sails of boats, the sweeping, constantly searching gulls. Desolation. Eternal, infinite. Like Dostoevsky’s conception of eternity, a fly circling about a privy, the few signs of life only emphasized the loneliness.”<br /><br />Okay, that’s not maybe quite Dostoevesky-level writing, it’s maybe “dimestore” Dostoevsky, but I don’t want to get all snobby about it, either; it is one of the virtues of noir that it combines sort of maudlin, over-the-top realism and poetry. <br /><br />Back to O’Brien’s contention about Greek themes, Roy has Oedipal-level feelings for his mother, which works out in an interesting way I will not discuss, since it very much involves the wonderful twists and turns of the ending to this short book that made me literally shout out loud with joy: whoo, what an ending, terrific. And is the book better than the movie? Yep! At the very least, it adds a layer of existential angst the movie leaves out.</span>
  <a data-text-id="566779342898069818" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_340256180" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/340256180" />
    <a title="Josh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1108056-josh"><img alt="Josh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1306470229p2/1108056.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/340256180?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/1108056-josh">Josh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780916870904" href="/book/show/7956686-the-grifters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1108056-josh?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1108056-josh?shelf=read_2012">read_2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1108056-josh?shelf=pulp">pulp</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer340256180" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14127714894236613246">As a grifter, Roy learnt from the best with his mother who was a master of the long con. For Roy, the short con has been prosperous, however its not without perils as we're shown quite early on in the story. It's this incident which brings back his mother, Lilly, and kicks off a chain of events which culminates in an ending nothing short of spectacular.<br/><br/>In order to reinstate his deteriorating health, Lilly commissions home nurse Carol which serves as a means to demonstrate Roy's mothers need to i</span>
  <span id="freeText14127714894236613246" style="display:none">As a grifter, Roy learnt from the best with his mother who was a master of the long con. For Roy, the short con has been prosperous, however its not without perils as we're shown quite early on in the story. It's this incident which brings back his mother, Lilly, and kicks off a chain of events which culminates in an ending nothing short of spectacular.<br /><br />In order to reinstate his deteriorating health, Lilly commissions home nurse Carol which serves as a means to demonstrate Roy's mothers need to influence him. As a character, she doesnt add all that much to the story, serving well as a Lilly's pawn rather than a point of focus in her own right. If anything, Carol highlights Roy's easy way with women and provides a nice correlation to the easy come easy go ways of his profession - Lilly, Carol, and girlfriend Moria are all important elements within Roy's games.<br /><br />The hint of forbidden attraction, the mere suggestion of the unthinkable, the reflection of desire in slippery sweet embrace of Moira, adds a complexity to Roy Dillon which isn't fully realised until the closing chapters. While bubbling under the surface, the tension and off-centre relationship between Roy and his young mother Lilly is paramount to proceedings, creating a story within a story, of which Moira is a key piece. <br /><br />'The Grifters' was a slow burn concluding with a blistering ending not to be missed. For a while I wasnt sure what all the hype surrounding Jim Thompson was about until the last 20 or so pages. Thompson weaved a web laden with influential and demanding characters supported by a plot of true grit and grift. If only the entire novel was as entertaining as the ending, this would've been hard to top. 4 stars. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14127714894236613246" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText11373675601930776066" style="display:none">I admit I watched the movie version of this book starring John Cusak a long time ago, well before I ever picked up the book, making me perhaps one of the last people on the planet to have read it. <div id="review_215334171" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/215334171" />
    <a title="Toby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5454475-toby"><img alt="Toby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542247337p2/5454475.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/215334171?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Toby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Toby" href="/user/show/5454475-toby">Toby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780752864280" href="/book/show/101221.The_Grifters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5454475-toby?shelf=lit">lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5454475-toby?shelf=black-as-night">black-as-night</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer215334171" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13846539528889442349">I find myself quite surprised at having never before read a Jim Thompson novel, I've known who he was and I've seen a lot of the movies based on his work, I've even owned a handful of his books for at least a year but still it took the pulp fiction group choosing it as the June read for me to actually pick one up.<br/><br/>Thompson has this reputation for being beyond dark, <b>holding up a circus mirror to life that only reflects the ugly, uncomfortable and depressing</b> and that is why I've found myself readin</span>
  <span id="freeText13846539528889442349" style="display:none">I find myself quite surprised at having never before read a Jim Thompson novel, I've known who he was and I've seen a lot of the movies based on his work, I've even owned a handful of his books for at least a year but still it took the pulp fiction group choosing it as the June read for me to actually pick one up.<br /><br />Thompson has this reputation for being beyond dark, <b>holding up a circus mirror to life that only reflects the ugly, uncomfortable and depressing</b> and that is why I've found myself reading my first Jim Thompson at 29 but my first Chandler at 17.<br /><br />The Grifters is an incredible book; immensely readable I struggled to stop myself from finishing it in one sitting, the three major players are fascinating in their own ways, backstories hinted at and fleshed out in equal measure and not a single redeeming feature in any of them. This being said they are still a joy to ride along with, you find yourself revelling in their meanness and amazed at what they are willing to do for a couple of bucks.<br /><br />The analysis of self destructive behaviour is done in such a way that pushes this novel from mere hard boiled pulp goodness in to serious literary territory without the obvious signs that mark so many try hard novelists as hacks. Thompson is the read deal, this much I can see after just one novel.<br /><br />I found myself amazed that it was written nearly 50 years ago as aside from a few minor details (mention of fleeing Nazi Germany and the obvious difference in monetary value thanks to inflation) this has a timeless feel and could easily be read as a modern day novel (as in fact Stephen Frears did with the movie.)<br /><br />Maybe I won't wait so long before reading my next Jim Thompson.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13846539528889442349" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_335651232" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/335651232" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4026072-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1458609397p2/4026072.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/335651232?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/4026072-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11380906-the-grifters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4026072-kim?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4026072-kim?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4026072-kim?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4026072-kim?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4026072-kim?shelf=male-author">male-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4026072-kim?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4026072-kim?shelf=noir">noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4026072-kim?shelf=1960s">1960s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4026072-kim?shelf=book-club-pulp-fiction">book-club-pulp-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer335651232" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12248366350808424049">The Grifters is the story of Roy Dillon con-artist specialising in short, quick scams, the son of Lilly, who is a con-srtist of a different type, working on horse betting, long-term scams.<br/><br/>After leaving home and traveling the country picking up money by the time the story kicks in Roy is based out of L.A. having successfully staying in one place long-term while still being able to run his cons. After a near brush with death Roy begins reconsidering his chosen life. At the same time he's dealing w</span>
  <span id="freeText12248366350808424049" style="display:none">The Grifters is the story of Roy Dillon con-artist specialising in short, quick scams, the son of Lilly, who is a con-srtist of a different type, working on horse betting, long-term scams.<br /><br />After leaving home and traveling the country picking up money by the time the story kicks in Roy is based out of L.A. having successfully staying in one place long-term while still being able to run his cons. After a near brush with death Roy begins reconsidering his chosen life. At the same time he's dealing with his mother and his sometimes girlfriend, Moira, an older woman who unbeknownst to Roy is also a con artist.<br /><br />It was interesting to read a noir novel based in the 60's and Roy was the perfect lead character plus two femme fatales. The ending was a great twist and the book was good from start to finish. I would have liked to have seen more of Roy doing his cons though and the whole Carol section was a little odd. It felt like an unfinished idea. I believe they dropped it from the film version and I can understand why. I really want to see the film now, I can't really picture John Cusack as Roy. A good book, worth reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12248366350808424049" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16180671173042545052" style="display:none">The Grifters is an exercise in animal behavior, specifically the reptilian overtures of homo sapiens. It is a feral book. What saves it, what elevates the narrative from the primordial is its kinetic codes of communication. The novel triumphs through its five or six principal conversations. The characters expand outside of type and blur our ready verdicts. There are human truths being issued from the mouths of vipers: assassins, certainly, but ones with souls.<br /><br />The film adaptation reveals the central set pieces, with one notable exception. It rained here this a.m. before the Manchester Derby, the solace of Roy Dillon was illuminating by contrast.<br /></span>

<div id="review_1765403039" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1765403039" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1765403039?book_show_action=true">Sep 22, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1765403039" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10702488863109271686">"The grift's like everything else. You don't stand still. You either go up or go down, usually down."<br/><br/><i>The Grifters</i> is a tidy hard-boiled book, dark and twisty and full of existential questions. Roy Dillon is the grifter son of a grifting woman, and the question is whether he's beyond redemption. Can he go straight? Can he fall in love and join the world? Jim Thompson is the darkest of the hard-boiled writers, so you know the odds are against him. But Thompson's also a master writer, so he sets it</span>
  <span id="freeText10702488863109271686" style="display:none">"The grift's like everything else. You don't stand still. You either go up or go down, usually down."<br /><br /><i>The Grifters</i> is a tidy hard-boiled book, dark and twisty and full of existential questions. Roy Dillon is the grifter son of a grifting woman, and the question is whether he's beyond redemption. Can he go straight? Can he fall in love and join the world? Jim Thompson is the darkest of the hard-boiled writers, so you know the odds are against him. But Thompson's also a master writer, so he sets it up cleanly and maybe there's hope.<br /><br />There's a subplot with a nurse named Carol who's Jewish, and I'm not sure where her sad history fits in. It felt like a tangent, and distracting. That bit had me wavering between three and four stars - that and the fact that I'm starting to have a sneaking feeling like, how many hard-boiled books does one need to read? They start to run together a little, with their bleak sensibilities and twisty plots. I've been assuming that I love noir, but I haven't really examined that; maybe I just like it.<br /><br />What sets Thompson apart - and he's one of the top five, no doubt - is, again, that he's willing to go darker. There's a torture scene in here that's very nasty; it goes beyond what other noir writers are willing to put on the page for you. I'm not saying that's a pro or con, just a difference. Dillon's relationship with his rough customer of a mom is nuts too: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[it very nearly tips over into incest, just before she murders him. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> The other book I've read by Thompson is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/306559009" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">The Killer Inside Me,</a> and I liked that better. But this is solid stuff; if you like noir / hard-boiled books, you'll like this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10702488863109271686" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3262128349" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3262128349" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7272380-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548615999p2/7272380.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/7272380-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mike by: </span>

  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7272380-mike?shelf=read-during-global-pandemic">read-during-global-pandemic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7272380-mike?shelf=the-60s">the-60s</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3262128349" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12332114512510120383"><br/><i>"Why shouldn't he take a job that he wanted to take? Why shouldn't a man want a friend, a real friend, when he has never before had one?"</i><br/><br/>The language in this novel is a pleasure. The descriptions, the dialogue. There must be something about the surname Thompson. This fella had a real way with words, as did Hunter.<br/><br/>They called Thompson, Jim that is, "the dimestore Dostoevsky" in his day, or so I hear, and in my book that's very high praise. On the strength of this novel, though, I'd have to say th</span>
  <span id="freeText12332114512510120383" style="display:none"><br /><i>"Why shouldn't he take a job that he wanted to take? Why shouldn't a man want a friend, a real friend, when he has never before had one?"</i><br /><br />The language in this novel is a pleasure. The descriptions, the dialogue. There must be something about the surname Thompson. This fella had a real way with words, as did Hunter.<br /><br />They called Thompson, Jim that is, "the dimestore Dostoevsky" in his day, or so I hear, and in my book that's very high praise. On the strength of this novel, though, I'd have to say that he earned it. Things seem to be under control at all times, and yet there's a real wildness to the story as well. It moves with a deceptive inevitability. There are haunting images (a burn mark on the back of a hand, hands around the neck of a sleeper in a dark hotel room). And then there's the hint of something on the periphery of the story that's truly perverse, that the main character isn't quite able to become conscious of, which makes it seem even more dreadful. Let's just say that I thought of Jim Morrison's greatest lyrical transgression. <br /><br />In his essay on Stanley Kubrick, Michael Herr gave his impression of Thompson: "Jim Thompson, the toughest pulp novelist of them all, had made [Stanley] nervous when they were working together on <i>The Killing</i>, a big guy in a dirty old raincoat, a terrific writer but a little too hard-boiled for Stanley’s taste. He’d turn up for work carrying a bottle in a brown paper bag, but saying nothing about it—it was just there on the desk with no apology or comment—not at all interested in putting Stanley at ease except to offer him the bag, which Stanley declined, and making no gestures whatever to any part of the Hollywood process, except maybe toward the money."</span>
  <a data-text-id="12332114512510120383" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer640330164" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18391643343044988604">As much as I cling to the insurmountable brilliance of Raymond Chandler, there is no doubt that the master of existential crime fiction is Jim Thompson.<br/><br/>His grasp and view of the human condition seems unnaturally sensitive and profound for someone who writes about such gritty environments populated by such edgy people.<br/><br/>The Grifters is a fascinating tale about Roy Dillon's life of grifting at a significant turning point in his life. Within its limited number of pages we are led to first accept Roy'</span>
  <span id="freeText18391643343044988604" style="display:none">As much as I cling to the insurmountable brilliance of Raymond Chandler, there is no doubt that the master of existential crime fiction is Jim Thompson.<br /><br />His grasp and view of the human condition seems unnaturally sensitive and profound for someone who writes about such gritty environments populated by such edgy people.<br /><br />The Grifters is a fascinating tale about Roy Dillon's life of grifting at a significant turning point in his life. Within its limited number of pages we are led to first accept Roy' current life and then begin to challenge it as it builds and crumbles established routines and relationships with ruthless efficiency.<br /><br />The criminal element to the plot is intriguing and surprising but not convoluted, giving some good twists and turns along the way whilst also serving as a vessel for a more personal story about a young lad coming to terms with his upbringing and where he wants his life to go on from there.<br /><br />Not quite "The Killer Inside Me", but another brilliant piece of work...</span>
  <a data-text-id="18391643343044988604" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText6263858625259963502" style="display:none">I have never read anybody who comes close to Thompson for his descriptions of violence, descriptions which in most books I skip for their tedium. <div id="review_2720922344" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2720922344" />
    <a title="Jamie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53587416-jamie"><img alt="Jamie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571965612p2/53587416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2720922344?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie" href="/user/show/53587416-jamie">Jamie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2720922344" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15051186728025576454">Through spot-on characterizations, thoughtful introspection and sharp dialogue, Thompson tells one hell of a story. While certainly dark, and with some disturbing themes, this ultimately comes off as an entertaining and oft-times amusing read. There is no honor among thieves, family and other relationships be damned. Grifters live on the edge, sucking off society for whatever they can get while keeping up appearances, and inevitably digging themselves in too deep. My only knock is that it was to</span>
  <span id="freeText15051186728025576454" style="display:none">Through spot-on characterizations, thoughtful introspection and sharp dialogue, Thompson tells one hell of a story. While certainly dark, and with some disturbing themes, this ultimately comes off as an entertaining and oft-times amusing read. There is no honor among thieves, family and other relationships be damned. Grifters live on the edge, sucking off society for whatever they can get while keeping up appearances, and inevitably digging themselves in too deep. My only knock is that it was too short. Highly recommended!</span>

<div id="review_3472110320" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3472110320" />
    <a title="Philip" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7413069-philip"><img alt="Philip" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583291726p2/7413069.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3472110320?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Philip" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Philip" href="/user/show/7413069-philip">Philip</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781619690066" href="/book/show/17560798-the-grifters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7413069-philip?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7413069-philip?shelf=noir">noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7413069-philip?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7413069-philip?shelf=filmed">filmed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7413069-philip?shelf=mystery-thriller-crime">mystery-thriller-crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7413069-philip?shelf=1960s">1960s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3472110320" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13760250157597394113">Okay, I think I am officially noir'ed out. Not even sure why I listened to this right now, after so much other recent noir stuff...maybe just available from the library? I forget.<br/><br/>But anyway...<b>The Grifters, The Reviews.<br/><br/>THE GRIFTERS, THE BOOK: </b>Not much of a real plot here, more like a character study of some consistently horrible characters. The only sympathetic person in the whole story is Roy's innocent Jewish nurse, who luckily gets herself extricated from the whole thing mid-story, although no</span>
  <span id="freeText13760250157597394113" style="display:none">Okay, I think I am officially noir'ed out. Not even sure why I listened to this right now, after so much other recent noir stuff...maybe just available from the library? I forget.<br /><br />But anyway...<b>The Grifters, The Reviews.<br /><br />THE GRIFTERS, THE BOOK: </b>Not much of a real plot here, more like a character study of some consistently horrible characters. The only sympathetic person in the whole story is Roy's innocent Jewish nurse, who luckily gets herself extricated from the whole thing mid-story, although not without considerable noir damage.<br /><br /><b>THE GRIFTERS, THE AUDIOBOOK: </b> Narrator Barbara Rosenblat is apparently a multi-award winning "voice artist," and has a deep, gravelly Kathleen Turner-esque voice that let her do a surprisingly good job with the various male characters here. But otherwise, she just acts the shit out of the dialogue throughout, which was really grating at first until I kinda just figured "what the hell," and gave into it. After that...not too bad; but nothing I would particularly recommend, and I certainly wouldn't go out of my way to look for her other work, (as I would for such stellar narrators as <b>The Andromeda Strain</b>'s David Morse or <b>Girl With All the Gifts</b>' Finty Williams).<br /><br /><b>THE GRIFTERS, THE MOVIE:</b> So also available from our library, and as I'd heard that this as one of the better Jim Thompson film adaptations, I watched this last night, same day as finishing the book in the car. And...<i>yikes</i>. Hard to believe that Scorsese produced this and Donald Westlake wrote the screenplay, because this either has aged <i>really poorly</i>, or was just terrible to begin with. Less an adaptation than a series of disjointed scenes from the book, this was just...<i>yikes</i>. And yet SOMEHOW this not only scored Oscar nominations for Westlake's screenplay, but also Best Actress (Anjelica Huston), Best Supporting Actress (Annette Benning) and Best Director (Stephen Frears) - on top of which it still scores a 91% on RT. So maybe it's just me, but to paraphrase Luther Ingram: "if hating this movie is wrong, I don't want to be right." (And okay, Benning was actually pretty good, but Huston was as laughably over-the-top as John Cusack was numbingly somnambulant.) <br /><br /><b>THE GRIFTERS, THE SCORE:</b> So. Probably 3.5 for Thompson's book; 3 for the audio version, and 1.5 for the movie - which, I know, shouldn't impact my score here, but, y'know...<i>YIKES</i>. So a borderline 3 overall...and no more noir!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13760250157597394113" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
