<span id="freeTextContainer1870952612674154911">I saw the movie all those years ago and thought it was amazing. Blew my mind. I had no idea it was based on a book. In my defense it was 1990. I was deep into my fantasy only phase. I am glad to have read the "source" material. I think it is darker than the movie. That is why books tend to be better, we get into the characters heads. I felt as I was reading there was a timeless quality. It could take place at almost anytime in history. People are people. My only issue with the book was Carol. I </span>
  <span id="freeText1870952612674154911" style="display:none">I saw the movie all those years ago and thought it was amazing. Blew my mind. I had no idea it was based on a book. In my defense it was 1990. I was deep into my fantasy only phase. I am glad to have read the "source" material. I think it is darker than the movie. That is why books tend to be better, we get into the characters heads. I felt as I was reading there was a timeless quality. It could take place at almost anytime in history. People are people. My only issue with the book was Carol. I don't remember how the movie treated Carol, but she seemed out of place. Out of time. I have been thinking about the author, his politics and views. Does she represent them? What does Carol mean. What kind of statement is she supposed to make. Am I reading to much into Carol. Perhaps Roy is simply a scoundrel and a bigot with no redeeming qualities after all....that was Carols job in the book to show us exactly who Roy was.<br /><br /> I suppose we should talk about Lily and Moria. What amazing women. I know they were ultimately liars and cheats. But at least they led their lives how they wanted in a time when that was frowned upon. I even felt a little bad for each of them. <br /><br />This is a raw, heartless story. I am glad to have read it. I now need to watch the movie. ( it is not available to stream, so I ordered the DVD)</span>

<div id="review_3128120270" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3128120270" />
    <a title="Lauren" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7852584-lauren"><img alt="Lauren" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468417220p2/7852584.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3128120270?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren" href="/user/show/7852584-lauren">Lauren</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7852584-lauren?shelf=crime">crime</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3128120270" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer295924667570579744">Roy Dillon makes his living off the short con--he bilks guys in bars out of their drinking money and little shops out of about twenty dollars a pop. None of it sounds like much, but as he explains, it has low-to-no overhead and it all mounts up. The problem with even the short con, however, is that it usually requires its operators stay mobile, always clearing out of one town for the police catch on. Roy doesn't like the way that drains his funds, however, so he's spent years building up a respe</span>
  <span id="freeText295924667570579744" style="display:none">Roy Dillon makes his living off the short con--he bilks guys in bars out of their drinking money and little shops out of about twenty dollars a pop. None of it sounds like much, but as he explains, it has low-to-no overhead and it all mounts up. The problem with even the short con, however, is that it usually requires its operators stay mobile, always clearing out of one town for the police catch on. Roy doesn't like the way that drains his funds, however, so he's spent years building up a respectable profile--and a friendly, upstanding personality to go along with it--and a legitimate career as a successful salesman. This way, he can be above suspicion if the police ever come knocking at his door. He thinks he's got it all figured out, until the day his "change for a twenty" scam ends with him getting hit in the stomach by a jumpy clerk. Roy hemorrhages, and that changes everything.<br /><br />For one thing, it brings his mother back into his life. Lilly Dillon knows the down-low life, too: she's a kind of Girl Friday for an outfit (and if someone can explain to me exactly what Lilly's doing with the track bets, you would have my thanks, because I couldn't follow that at all), respected by her boss... but not in a way that will save her from taking a lit cigarette to the back of her hand when she screws up. (By the standards of their business, she's getting off lightly.) Lilly and Roy have never had a good--or normal--relationship. She was only a teenager when she had him, and she tended to view him as a burden right up until he hit his own teenage years, whereupon a weird, queasy sexual attraction started being a problem for them both.<br /><br />Lilly saves Roy's life, but she stirs it up, too, aggravating his girlfriend, Moira, who has fading schemes and vivid ambitions of her own, and bringing Roy into collision with the innocent Carol, a nurse hired for Roy's convalescence. The book has been compulsively readable until Carol shows up, but it's with her appearance that Thompson gets into something darker and more revelatory. Carol's circumstances throw the other characters' machinations into stark relief--she exists, effectively, on a moral level, and a level of significance, beyond and above them, with a dignity that they can't even aspire to, and even as Roy treats her badly, she has an effect on him, making him feel something of the smallness and sordidness of the life he's been living. But Roy's growing, if ambivalent, desire to go clean--as long as he can also stay rich--is all set up to collide with Lilly's desperation and Moira's plans for escalation.<br /><br />The novel turns into a dark, uneasy love triangle--Moira, Roy, and Lilly--that ultimately offers a kind of grim and inexorable tragedy. Roy's life may have been small, and his soul may have been shallow, but Thompson succeeds in making something big out of it. The story of <i>The Grifters</i> offers all the depth its characters mostly lack, and the ending hits hard.</span>
  <a data-text-id="295924667570579744" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_280003239" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/280003239" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4015835-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420385695p2/4015835.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/280003239?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/4015835-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11380906-the-grifters">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=pulp">pulp</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=noir">noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=book-club-others">book-club-others</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=crime">crime</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer280003239" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10918867434220897115">There is a reason they call Jim Thompson the Dimestore Dostoevsky; his works really spotlight the moral dilemma his protagonists and main characters face, so I was really looking forward to reading The Grifters; I’ve seen the movie so I was interested in seen the inner thoughts of the characters. 25-year-old short con operator Roy Dillion suffers an injury when a simple con goes horribly wrong, he finds himself in hospital recovering from an internal haemorrhage. This brush with death has led hi</span>
  <span id="freeText10918867434220897115" style="display:none">There is a reason they call Jim Thompson the Dimestore Dostoevsky; his works really spotlight the moral dilemma his protagonists and main characters face, so I was really looking forward to reading The Grifters; I’ve seen the movie so I was interested in seen the inner thoughts of the characters. 25-year-old short con operator Roy Dillion suffers an injury when a simple con goes horribly wrong, he finds himself in hospital recovering from an internal haemorrhage. This brush with death has led him to rethink his life, thought his mother Lilly feels like Roy still owes her. She’s inattentive and manipulative while trying to care for Roy, but she is also trying to pull off a long con at the race tracks at the same time. Throw in another femme fatale; Moira, Roy’s girlfriend who also find out is also a grifter who favours the long con. The three explosive characters make for an interesting and twisted noir story, much to what we have come to expect from Jim Thompson. <br /><br />I have to admit I do love Jim Thompson’s twisted plotting, he captures the pulp feel well while giving it is own flavour of surrealism. While The Grifters is not is most solid piece it does a good job at spotlighting what this author can do, with crime and with is unreliable narrating. The characters are great and they each work well together while making life difficult for each other. His is one of the few pulp authors that break into the world of serious literary while never losing sight of what he does best. <br /><br />If you haven’t read a Jim Thompson book maybe this is a good place to start, it’s not as dark or gritty, they characters are great, the plot isn’t as twisted as some of his other works and the suspense is a bit watered down. Let’s just call this book ‘Jim Thompson for beginners’ it gives you everything you expect in a Thompson book, just not to the same intensity while making the book approachable and easy to read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10918867434220897115" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2213185420" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11560245587255683197">people who want action without deep psychological understanding should probably skip Thompson, at least until they're had more experience of life, because that's what he's writing about, not tricks and shoot-outs. one of his later but not lesser works. he was 57 when he published GRIFTERS in 1963. the story of a Mother with a capital M for malevolent. such stories are rare but earthshaking. a triangle between a man, his older girlfriend, and his Mother, whose hearts are dead. short, fast read, b</span>
  <span id="freeText11560245587255683197" style="display:none">people who want action without deep psychological understanding should probably skip Thompson, at least until they're had more experience of life, because that's what he's writing about, not tricks and shoot-outs. one of his later but not lesser works. he was 57 when he published GRIFTERS in 1963. the story of a Mother with a capital M for malevolent. such stories are rare but earthshaking. a triangle between a man, his older girlfriend, and his Mother, whose hearts are dead. short, fast read, but every detail carefully chosen. he knows exactly where he's taking you and why. the downward spiral of a man who's losing his touch. devastating. a small oedipal tragedy that accuses the bankrupt ideals of USA.<br /><br />the 1998 film was a chance missed, Frears the wrong director. Anjelica Huston perfectly cast as Mother, but her co-stars wimps. i saw the film before i read the book and compared them mentally scene by scene. the film reorganizes the material in ways that kill the build, excises important backstory, and redundantly shows certain tricks over and over again. bad bad screenplay. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11560245587255683197" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('T5XslZO2rA04qMFC8TINoNuYunXKbidER2cq3I1ZVK/LCmC+i5zuOXeSa2bBzWdDgYRnyT3FX5dX5Krs8W9+TQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('OBMor8K/hSS0Gmm1dFI9qYGng3QCiwNJQYG6Db8tuym8jKSE2pXHEPsgw5FErVdK27teyPUge5pRAjo9wxuRyw==')})
  }
//]]>

The more Harry succeeds -- a good marriage, a good corporate job -- the more desperate he becomes, as a life of petty crime leads to fraud and murder and, eventually, to apocalyptic violence.Author of the controversial cult classic, Last Exit to Brooklyn, Hubert Selby began as a writer of short fiction. He plunges the reader head-first into the densely realized worlds of his protagonists, in which the details of daily life rub shoulders with obsession and madness. Although fundamentally concerned with morality, Selby\'s own sense of humility prevents him from preaching.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7621.Jim_Thompson">About Jim Thompson</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/7621.Jim_Thompson"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1360741132p3/7621.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/7621.Jim_Thompson">Jim Thompson
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,772
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3528846928686906506"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the GoodReads database with this name. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/44217-master-list-of-author-disambiguation-by-spaces' rel='nofollow noopener'>See this thread for more information.</a></i> <br/><br/>James Myers Thompson was a United States writer of novels, short stories and screenplays, largely in the hardboiled style of crime fiction.<br/><br/>Thompson wrote more than thirty novels, the majority of which were original paperback publications by pulp fiction houses, from the lat</span>
  <span id="freeText3528846928686906506" style="display:none"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the GoodReads database with this name. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/44217-master-list-of-author-disambiguation-by-spaces" rel="nofollow noopener">See this thread for more information.</a></i> <br /><br />James Myers Thompson was a United States writer of novels, short stories and screenplays, largely in the hardboiled style of crime fiction.<br /><br />Thompson wrote more than thirty novels, the majority of which were original paperback publications by pulp fiction houses, from the late-1940s through mid-1950s. Despite some positive critical notice, notably by Anthony Boucher in the <i>New York Times</i>, he was little-recognized in his lifetime. Only after death did Thompson's literary stature grow, when in the late 1980s, several novels were re-published in the <i>Black Lizard</i> series of re-discovered crime fiction.<br /><br />Thompson's writing culminated in a few of his best-regarded works: <i>The Killer Inside Me, Savage Night, A Hell of a Woman</i> and <i>Pop. 1280</i>. In these works, Thompson turned the derided pulp genre into literature and art, featuring unreliable narrators, odd structure, and surrealism.<br /><br />The writer R.V. Cassills has suggested that of all pulp fiction, Thompson's was the rawest and most harrowing; that neither Dashiell Hammett nor Raymond Chandler nor even Horace McCoy, author of the bleak <i>They Shoot Horses, Don't They?</i>, ever "wrote a book within miles of Thompson". Similarly, in the introduction to Now and on Earth, Stephen King says he most admires Thompson's work because "The guy was over the top. The guy was absolutely over the top. Big Jim didn't know the meaning of the word stop. There are three brave lets inherent in the forgoing: he let himself see everything, he let himself write it down, then he let himself publish it."<br /><br />Thompson admired Fyodor Dostoevsky and was nicknamed "Dimestore Dostoevsky" by writer Geoffrey O'Brien. Film director Stephen Frears, who directed an adaptation of Thompson's The Grifters as 1990's <i>The Grifters</i>, also identified elements of Greek tragedy in his themes.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3528846928686906506" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
